NAsBVILLE, January 1, 1908. 
Zo His Excellency, M. R. Patterson, Governor of Tennessee: 
Srr—In compliance with the provisions of Section 7, Sub- 
section 12, of the Acts of 1873, as amended by Chapter 153, 
Section 17, of the Acts of 1907, I have the honor to submit to 
your Excellency the statistical report of this department for 
the scholastic year ending June 30, 1907. 
Very respectfuily, . 
R. L. Jonzs, 
State Superintendent.

STATE SUPERINTENDENTS OF PUBLIC 
INSTRUCTION. 
‘The office of State Superintendent of Public Instruction was 
created in 1835, and by an Act of the Legislature of 1843, 
which went into effect in 1844, the office was abolished. The 
following Superintendents were elected by the Legislature: 
Rozsert H. McEwen, 1835-1840. 
Rosert P. Currin, 1840-1841. 
Scort Terry, 1841-1844. 
From 1844 to 1867 the State Treasurer was ex-officio Su- 
perintendent of Public Instruction. In 1867 the office was 
again created. The following Superintendents were elected 
by the people, and served for the respective periods shown 
below: . 
Joun Eaton, Jr., 1867-1869. 
A. J. Tipton, 1869-1871. 
In 1870 the office was again abolished, and the duties de- 
volved upon the State Treasurer, who was made ex-officio 
Superintendent of Public Instruction. 
In 1873 the office was recreated, and the following officers, 
appointed by the Governor and confirmed by the Senate, have 
served the terms shown below: 
*J, B. KInLeprew. 
Joun M. Fiemine, 1873-1875. 
Luon TRrousDALz, 1875-1881. 
TW. S. Doax, 1881-1882. 
IG. S. W. Crawrorp, 1882-1883. 
Tomas H. Parnz, 1883-1887. 
Frank M. Smrru, 1887-1891. 
W. R. Garrerr, 1891-1893, 
" Frank M. Surru, 1893-1895. 
8. G. GILBREATH, 1895-1897. 
Pricr THomas, 1897-1899, 
Morean C., Firzparrick, 1899-1903. 
Seymour A. Mynpers, 1903-1907, 
R. L. Jonus, 1907, present incumbent. 
* Assistant State Superintendent, 1870-1873. 
+ Died in office. 
t Filled unexpired tert.

DEPARTMENT OF PUBLIC INSTRUCTION 
NasuviLiy, TENN., January 1, 1908. 
To His Excellency, M. R. Patterson, Governor of Tennessee: 
In compliance with Section 7, Subsection 12 of the Acts 
of 1873, as amended by Chapter 153, Section 17 of the Acts 
of 1907, I herewith submit the annual statistical report of 
this department for the scholastic year ending June 30, 1907. 
It is proper to state that I have been unable to obtain in- 
formation for some of the items contained in this report, 
owing to the fact that the revision of the school law made it 
necessary to secure statistics which had not heretofore been 
furnished, and in many instances the records kept by the 
County and City Superintendents were not sufficient to fur- 
nish the figures requested. 
While Ihave prepared tables showing a comparison of 
the school years ending June 30, 1906, and June 30, 1907, I 
desire to call attention to some of the features which indicate 
the progress made during the scholastic year ending June 
30, 1907 
’ By the provisions of the school law as amended in 1907, 
not only the name and age of the child, but the name of the 
parent or guardian of each child was taken by the clerk of 
the district and the report shows a decrease in the scholastic 
population in the whole State of more than 12,000. ‘This 
falling off was due to the fact that the reports made under 
the new law are more nearly accurate than ever before. In 
Table II the number of pupils enrolled under the head of ‘‘Who 
Can Read and Write,” is 346,992, but 23 counties with a 
scholastic population of 269,854 did not report for this table; 
so that 346,992 children out of a scholastic population of 
476,771 can ‘“‘ read and write,” or about 70 percent. I think 
this percentage will hold good as to those counties which 
did not file reports. , , 
Under the head of the ‘‘ Number of Pupils Enrolled,” the 
enrollment on June 30, 1907, is more’ than 10,000 less than
-P-A-G-E-29
—23— 
the number enrolled in 1906, yet the average daily attendance . 
in 1907 shows an increase over 1906. Ejfforts have been made 
for the past several years to interest the children of the State 
in the study of Elementary Principles of Agriculture, and 
Table VI indicates that the number of pupils enrolled in this 
branch of study on June 30, 1907, is more than double the 
number enrolled in any previous year. 
Table VII indicates that while the total number of schools 
in the State has decreased, yet the number of secondary and 
city schools has increased, and this with the fact that the num- 
ber of teachers employed has increased is evidence that small 
schools employing one teacher have been consolidated and 
secondary schools employing several teachers have been 
established instead. ‘‘ The Estimated Value of School Prop- 
erty” shows an increase of more than $400,000 for 1907 over 
the previous year, which means that the people are awaken- 
ing to the necessity of better school buildings. One of the 
best showings made is indicated in Table Xlin which the 
average nuinber of days taught and the average compensation 
of each teacher has increased, yet thé average cost of tuition 
per month per pupil has decreased. 
For the past three or four years the question of the estab- 
lishment of county high schools has been agitated over the 
State and as a result the number of diplomas issued to high 
school graduates for the year ending June 30, 1907, is almost 
double the number of the. year preceding.. While the question 
of libraries for rural schools has been considered by only a few 
of the county officials, yet under Table XII items 98 to 103, 
inclusive, give information not heretofore compiled, showing 
that at this time there are more than 50,000 volumes in our 
public school libraries of the State, and I hope to be able to 
announce in my next report that this number has been more 
than doubled. : . 
A discouraging feature in our public school system is 
indicated in Table XIII, in which the number of male teach- 
ers has decreased about 300. Men are giving up the profes- 
sion of school teaching on account of low salaries and short 
terms and are seeking more remunerative avocations, and the 
places left vacant by them are frequently filled by young and 
inexperienced ladies. No business can show its best results 
unless its employes are paid a sufficient compensation to
-P-A-G-E-30
—29— 
insure the best talent. Considering the fact that we have 
no State Normal School for teachers, the increase of first 
grade certificates from 1,552 to 1,715 is creditable, and indi- 
dicates that.the teachers of the State realize the necessity 
for better equipment for their work. The blanks calling for 
report of Receipts and Disbursements have been rearranged 
and in Tables XIV and XV the different items and the 
amounts for which the school money for the State has been 
expended are pointed out, and while several hundred books 
have been purchased for libraries, and the salaries of a large 
number of County Superintendents and teachers have been 
increased, yet the total amount expended is about the same 
as the preceding year. This is due in a large measure toa 
more economical management of the school funds in the 
counties, 
The private schools and city schools of the State show a 
substantial gain in value of school property, number of teach- 
ers, equipments and number of pupils enrolled. 
Respectfully submitted, 
R. L. Jonzs, 
State Superintendent.
-P-A-G-E-31

-P-A-G-E-32
